<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome of the tropical plant Marchantia inflexa : implications for sex chromosome evolution and dehydration tolerance"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2019 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-06-19"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="We present a draft genome assembly for the tropical liverwort, Marchantia inflexa, which adds to a growing body of genomic resources for bryophytes and provides an important perspective on the evolution and diversification of land plants. We specifically address questions related to sex chromosome evolution, sexual dimorphisms, and the genomic underpinnings of dehydration tolerance. This assembly leveraged the recently published genome of related liverwort, M. polymorpha, to improve scaffolding and annotation, aid in the identification of sex-linked sequences, and quantify patterns of sequence differentiation within Marchantia. We find that genes on sex chromosomes are under greater diversifying selection than autosomal and organellar genes. Interestingly, this is driven primarily by divergence of male-specific genes, while divergence of other sex-linked genes is similar to autosomal genes. Through analysis of sex-specific read coverage, we identify and validate genetic sex markers for M. inflexa, which will enable diagnosis of sex for non-reproductive individuals. To investigate dehydration tolerance, we capitalized on a difference between genetic lines, which allowed us to identify multiple dehydration associated genes two of which were sex-linked, suggesting that dehydration tolerance may be impacted by sex-specific genes."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-2705-4872"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-2705-4872</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of British Columbia" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2288 9830, grid.17091.3e, Department of Botany, University of British Columbia, 6270 University Boulevard, Vancouver, BC, V6T 1Z4, Canada" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden" data-present-affiliation="true"><meta itemprop="name" content="Harvard University Herbaria" /><meta itemprop="address" content="000000041936754X, grid.38142.3c, Department of Organismic and Evolutionary Biology, Harvard University Herbaria, 22 Divinity Avenue, Cambridge, MA, 02138, USA" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide">Â <a href="#nAff3">nAff3</a></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-D__Nicholas-McLetchie" data-author-popup="auth-D__Nicholas-McLetchie">D. Nicholas McLetchie</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Kentucky" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 8438, grid.266539.d, Department of Biology, University of Kentucky, 101 Thomas Hunt Morgan Building, Lexington, KY, 40506, USA" /></span></sup>Â </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>We present a draft genome assembly for the tropical liverwort, <i>Marchantia inflexa</i>, which adds to a growing body of genomic resources for bryophytes and provides an important perspective on the evolution and diversification of land plants. We specifically address questions related to sex chromosome evolution, sexual dimorphisms, and the genomic underpinnings of dehydration tolerance. This assembly leveraged the recently published genome of related liverwort, <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>, to improve scaffolding and annotation, aid in the identification of sex-linked sequences, and quantify patterns of sequence differentiation within <i>Marchantia</i>. We find that genes on sex chromosomes are under greater diversifying selection than autosomal and organellar genes. Interestingly, this is driven primarily by divergence of male-specific genes, while divergence of other sex-linked genes is similar to autosomal genes. Through analysis of sex-specific read coverage, we identify and validate genetic sex markers for <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>, which will enable diagnosis of sex for non-reproductive individuals. To investigate dehydration tolerance, we capitalized on a difference between genetic lines, which allowed us to identify multiple dehydration associated genes two of which were sex-linked, suggesting that dehydration tolerance may be impacted by sex-specific genes.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Bryophytes (mosses, liverworts, and hornworts) are living representatives of an early diverging land plant lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kenrick, P. &amp; Crane, P. R. The origin and early evolution of plants on land. Nature 389, 33â39 (1997)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d65126e476">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304.e15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65126e479">2</a></sup> and they provide an important landmark for comparative phylogenetics. Although the exact relationships among bryophyte lineages are currently contested, the earliest fossil evidence assigned to a bryophyte is liverwort-like with dorsiventral complex thallus morphology and a leafless gametophyte<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ligrone, R., Duckett, J. G. &amp; Renzaglia, K. S. Major transitions in the evolution of early land plants: a bryological perspective. Ann. Bot. 109, 851â71 (2012)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d65126e483">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wellman, C. H., Osterloff, P. L. &amp; Mohiuddin, U. Fragments of the earliest land plants. Nature 425, 282â285 (2003)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d65126e483_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 115, E2274âE2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d65126e486">5</a></sup>, suggesting that liverworts retain a large suite of ancestral characters not conserved in other land plants. Importantly, building a fundamental understanding of genomic patterns can be readily accomplished by working with bryophytes because of their small genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Leitch, I. J. &amp; Leitch, A. R. Genome size diversity and evolution in land plants. in Plant Genome Diversity (eds Jones, R. N. &amp; Langdon, T.) 307â322, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-7091-1160-4_2&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (Springer, 2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d65126e490">6</a></sup>, many of which contain comparatively few paralogous duplications of regulatory genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304.e15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65126e494">2</a></sup>. Thus far, genome assemblies have been developed for a number of bryophyte species (including the mosses <i>Physcomitrella patens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rensing, S. A. et al. The Physcomitrella genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science (80-.). 319, 64â9 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d65126e500">7</a></sup>, and <i>Sphagnum fallax</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Shaw, A. J. et al. The Sphagnum Genome Project. in 167â187, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1016/bs.abr.2016.01.003&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d65126e507">8</a></sup>, the liverwort <i>Marchantia polymorpha</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304.e15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65126e513">2</a></sup>, and the hornwort <i>Anthoceros agrestis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="SzÃ¶vÃ©nyi, P. The Genome of the Model Species Anthoceros agrestis. Adv. Bot. Res. 78, 189â211 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d65126e519">9</a></sup>), and more are underway (<i>Takakia lepidozioides</i>, <i>Ceratodon purpureus</i>, <i>Funaria hygrometrica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Rensing, S. A. Why we need more non-seed plant models. New Phytol. 216, 355â360 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d65126e532">10</a></sup> and <i>Syntricia caninervis</i>). Relatively few assemblies of bryophyte mitochondria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Oda, K. et al. Gene organization deduced from the complete sequence of liverwort Marchantia polymorpha mitochondrial DNA. A primitive form of plant mitochondrial genome. J. Mol. Biol. 223, 1â7 (1992)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d65126e539">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Terasawa, K. et al. The Mitochondrial Genome of the Moss Physcomitrella patens Sheds New Light on Mitochondrial Evolution in Land Plants. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 699â709 (2007)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65126e539_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, B., Xue, J., Li, L., Liu, Y. &amp; Qiu, Y.-L. The complete mitochondrial genome sequence of the liverwort Pleurozia purpurea reveals extremely conservative mitochondrial genome evolution in liverworts. Curr. Genet. 55, 601â609 (2009)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d65126e539_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, L., Wang, B., Liu, Y. &amp; Qiu, Y.-L. The Complete Mitochondrial Genome Sequence of the Hornwort Megaceros aenigmaticus Shows a Mixed Mode of Conservative Yet Dynamic Evolution in Early Land Plant Mitochondrial Genomes. J. Mol. Evol. 68, 665â678 (2009)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65126e539_3">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="MyszczyÅski, K. et al. The complete mitochondrial genome of the cryptic species C of Aneura pinguis. Mitochondrial DNA Part A 28, 112â113 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d65126e542">15</a></sup> and chloroplasts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sugiura, C., Kobayashi, Y., Aoki, S., Sugita, C. &amp; Sugita, M. Complete chloroplast DNA sequence of the moss Physcomitrella patens: evidence for the loss and relocation of rpoA from the chloroplast to the nucleus. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5324â31 (2003)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d65126e546">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kugita, M., Yamamoto, Y., Fujikawa, T., Matsumoto, T. &amp; Yoshinaga, K. RNA editing in hornwort chloroplasts makes more than half the genes functional. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 2417â23 (2003)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d65126e546_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Wolf, P. G. et al. The first complete chloroplast genome sequence of a lycophyte, Huperzia lucidula (Lycopodiaceae). Gene 350, 117â128 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d65126e549">18</a></sup> are available compared to other plant lineages, yet mitochondrial genomes of bryophytes tend to be less complex than those of tracheophytes, having no large repeated sequences and limited recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ohyama, K. Chloroplast and Mitochondrial Genomes from a Liverwort, Marchantia polymorpha âGene Organization and Molecular Evolutionâ. Biosci. Biotechnol. Biochem. 60, 16â24 (1996)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d65126e554">19</a></sup>. Genome sequencing efforts of additional bryophyte taxa will provide critical insight into more recent evolutionary changes within these lineages and may help to better resolve ancestral states.</p><p>In this context, we targeted the tropical liverwort, <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> (Nees &amp; Mont) for sequencing and assembly. <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> is a New World liverwort that is distributed throughout Central America and the Caribbean, from northern Venezuela to the southern United States<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Bischler, H. &amp; Marchantia, L. The New World Species (Bryophytorum Bibliotheca, Band 26). (Lubrecht &amp; Cramer Ltd, 1984)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65126e567">20</a></sup>. <i>Marchantia inflexa is</i> 68â126 million years diverged from the well-studied sister species <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Suleski, M. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree: A Resource for Timelines, Timetrees, and Divergence Times. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1812â1819 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d65126e580">21</a></sup>. <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> is dioecious (has unisexual individuals), has eight autosomes and one female (U) or male (V) sex chromosome, and reproduces sexually by spores, or asexually by fragmentation or the formation of gemmae (specialized asexual propagules). <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> typically grows on rock and soil surfaces along stream banks in tropical forests, but can also colonize more exposed and disturbed sites along roads. Vegetative growth produces a dichotomously branching thallus mat with dorsiventral organization, and the haploid gametophyte is the dominant life phase. <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> is a useful model to investigate sexual dimorphisms, population sex ratios, and stress tolerance because prior work has established that <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> exhibits a considerable degree of sexual dimorphism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="McLetchie, D. N. &amp; Puterbaugh, M. N. Population sex ratios, sex-specific clonal traits and tradeoffs among these traits in the liverwort Marchantia inflexa. Oikos 90, 227â237 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d65126e600">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Brzyski, J. R., Taylor, W. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Reproductive allocation between the sexes, across natural and novel habitats, and its impact on genetic diversity. Evol. Ecol. 28, 247â261 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d65126e603">23</a></sup>, variable population sex ratios<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="McLetchie, D. N. &amp; Puterbaugh, M. N. Population sex ratios, sex-specific clonal traits and tradeoffs among these traits in the liverwort Marchantia inflexa. Oikos 90, 227â237 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d65126e607">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="McLetchie, D. N. &amp; GarcÃ­a-Ramos, G. A predictive relationship between population and genetic sex ratios in clonal species. Acta Oecologica 80, 18â23 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d65126e610">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Brzyski, J. R., Stieha, C. R. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. The impact of asexual and sexual reproduction in spatial genetic structure within and between populations of the dioecious plant Marchantia inflexa (Marchantiaceae). Ann. Bot, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/aob/mcy106&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d65126e613">25</a></sup>, and fluctuating stress tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e617">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Stieha, C. R., Middleton, A. R., Stieha, J. K., Trott, S. H. &amp; Mcletchie, D. N. The dispersal process of asexual propagules and the contribution to population persistence in Marchantia (Marchantiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 101, 348â356 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65126e620">27</a></sup>.</p><p>Bryophytes harbor a high proportion of dioecious species. Nearly half of all extant mosses, and approximately two-thirds of liverworts are dioecious<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wyatt, R. &amp; Anderson, L. E. Breeding systems in bryophytes. Exp. Biol. Bryophyt./Ed. by A.F. Dye. J.G. Duckett (1984)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d65126e627">28</a></sup>. In many bryophytes (including <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>) the reported sex ratio is often female biased<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="McLetchie, D. N. &amp; GarcÃ­a-Ramos, G. A predictive relationship between population and genetic sex ratios in clonal species. Acta Oecologica 80, 18â23 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d65126e637">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Brzyski, J. R., Stieha, C. R. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. The impact of asexual and sexual reproduction in spatial genetic structure within and between populations of the dioecious plant Marchantia inflexa (Marchantiaceae). Ann. Bot, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/aob/mcy106&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d65126e640">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Marks, R. A., Smith, J. J., Cronk, Q. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Variation in the bacteriome of the tropical liverwort, Marchantia inflexa, between the sexes and across habitats. Symbiosis 1â9, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s13199-017-0522-3&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d65126e643">29</a></sup>, and in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>, this may be related to femalesâ superior ability to recover from drying events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e654">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Stieha, C. R., Middleton, A. R., Stieha, J. K., Trott, S. H. &amp; Mcletchie, D. N. The dispersal process of asexual propagules and the contribution to population persistence in Marchantia (Marchantiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 101, 348â356 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65126e657">27</a></sup>, faster growth rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Brzyski, J. R., Taylor, W. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Reproductive allocation between the sexes, across natural and novel habitats, and its impact on genetic diversity. Evol. Ecol. 28, 247â261 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d65126e661">23</a></sup>, or the increased establishment of female gemmae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Stieha, C. R., Middleton, A. R., Stieha, J. K., Trott, S. H. &amp; Mcletchie, D. N. The dispersal process of asexual propagules and the contribution to population persistence in Marchantia (Marchantiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 101, 348â356 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65126e665">27</a></sup>. However, true population sex ratios are largely unknown, except for the few cases where genetic sex markers have been developed and utilized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McLetchie, D. N. &amp; Collins, A. L. Identification of DNA Regions Specific to the X and Y Chromosomes in Sphaerocarpos texanus. Bryologist 104, 543â547 (2001)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d65126e669">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Korpelainen, H., Bisang, I., Hedenas, L. &amp; Kolehmainen, J. The First Sex-Specific Molecular Marker Discovered in the Moss Pseudocalliergon trifarium. J. Hered. 99, 581â587 (2008)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d65126e669_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Milewicz, M. &amp; Sawicki, akub. Sex-linked markers in dioecious plants. Plant Omi. 6, 144â149 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65126e672">32</a></sup>. Typical methods for assessing sex ratios depend on counting the number males and females with visible sex organs and using this to infer the underlying population sex ratio. However, this approach fails to account for plants not currently displaying sex organs and assumes that the sex ratio of vegetative plants is equivalent to that of plants with sex organs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="HolÃ¡, E., Vesalainen, T., TÄÅ¡itel, J. &amp; Laaka-Lindberg, S. Sex ratio, sex-specific pattern in vegetative growth and gemma production in an aquatic liverwort, Scapania undulata (Marchantiophyta: Scapaniaceae). Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 175, 229â241 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d65126e676">33</a></sup>. This assumption may not hold true in natural settings. In fact, for both <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> (where sex organ development can be artificially induced) the timing of reproductive development is sex-specific<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Wann, F. B. Some of the Factors Involved in the Sexual Reproduction of Marchantia polymorpha. Am. J. Bot. 12, 307 (1925)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d65126e693">34</a></sup> and some individuals never produce sex organs (unpublished data).</p><p>The reproductive biology of bryophytes (with the haploid gametophyte being the dominant life stage) provides a unique perspective on the evolution of sex-linked genes, as the female (U) and male (V) sex chromosomes are present at the same copy number (1N) as autosomal chromosomes for the majority of the organismâs life cycle and are subject to haploid selection. Sex chromosome evolution in diploid dominant systems has received considerable research attention. However, less is known about the forces shaping sex chromosomes in haploid dominant systems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bachtrog, D. et al. Are all sex chromosomes created equal? Trends Genet. 27, 350â357 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d65126e700">35</a></sup>, and the ramifications of haploid selection on sex chromosomes may have unique consequences. For example, exposure to haploid selection should reduce the prevalence of deleterious mutations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Immler, S. &amp; Otto, S. P. The evolution of sex chromosomes in organisms with separate haploid sexes. Evolution (N. Y). 69, 694â708 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d65126e704">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Bull, J. J. Sex Chromosomes in Haploid Dioecy: A Unique Contrast to Mullerâs Theory for Diploid Dioecy. Am. Nat. 112, 245â250 (1978)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d65126e707">37</a></sup> and could allow beneficial mutations fix more rapidly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Immler, S. &amp; Otto, S. P. The evolution of sex chromosomes in organisms with separate haploid sexes. Evolution (N. Y). 69, 694â708 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d65126e711">36</a></sup>. However, lack of recombination on UV sex chromosomes could lead to degeneration on UV chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bachtrog, D. et al. Are all sex chromosomes created equal? Trends Genet. 27, 350â357 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d65126e715">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Immler, S. &amp; Otto, S. P. The evolution of sex chromosomes in organisms with separate haploid sexes. Evolution (N. Y). 69, 694â708 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d65126e718">36</a></sup>, as has been observed in XY and ZW chromosomes. Further, the smaller effective population size of sex chromosomes relative to autosomes may increase the impact of genetic drift, further influencing adaptive evolution of sex-specific genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="McDaniel, S. F., Neubig, K. M., Payton, A. C., Quatrano, R. S. &amp; Cove, D. J. Recent gene-capture on the UV sex chromosomes of the moss Ceratodon purpureus. Evolution (N. Y). 67 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d65126e722">38</a></sup>. The extent to which these forces shape sex chromosome evolution in haploid dominant systems is not well understood, but the numerous dioecious bryophyte taxa provide novel opportunities to test related questions.</p><p>Stresses caused by environmental fluctuations are accentuated in plants due to their sessile nature. Consequently, numerous tolerance mechanisms have evolved to combat environmental pressures, many of which have potential translational utility. Some of these stress tolerance traits, such as embryo retention (allowing for the development and dispersal of desiccation tolerant spores), UV radiation, desiccation, heat, and freezing tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304.e15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65126e730">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Rensing, S. A. Great moments in evolution: the conquest of land by plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 42, 49â54 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d65126e733">39</a></sup> may have facilitated the transition from aquatic to terrestrial environments by early plants. Many extant bryophytes retain these early stress tolerance mechanisms, allowing them to occupy marginal niches (characterized by nutrient poor substrates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Turetsky, M. R. The Role of Bryophytes in Carbon and Nitrogen Cycling. Bryologist 106, 395â409 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d65126e737">40</a></sup>, toxic concentrations of metals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Shaw, J. Evolution of Heavy Metal Tolerance in Bryophytes. II. An Ecological and Experimental Investigation of the Copper Moss Scopelophila cataractae (Pottiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 74, 813 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d65126e741">41</a></sup>, variable light<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="van der Wal, R., Pearce, I. S. K. &amp; Brooker, R. W. Mosses and the struggle for light in a nitrogen-polluted world. Oecologia 142, 159â168 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d65126e745">42</a></sup> and moisture levels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Proctor, M. C. F. et al. Desiccation-tolerance in bryophytes: a review Desiccation-tolerance in bryophytes: a review. Bryologist 110, 595â621 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d65126e749">43</a></sup>). Consequently, bryophytes are particularly informative with respect to understanding the evolutionary history and physiological strategies of stress tolerance.</p><p>Desiccation tolerance (DT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Proctor, M. C. F. et al. Desiccation-tolerance in bryophytes: a review Desiccation-tolerance in bryophytes: a review. Bryologist 110, 595â621 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d65126e756">43</a></sup> in particular, has important translational utility. A number of studies have described the genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Costa, M.-C. D. et al. A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of Xerophyta viscosa. Nat. Plants 3, 17038 (2017)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d65126e760">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xiao, L. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Notes 7, 490 (2014)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d65126e767_2">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yobi, A. et al. Plant J. 63, 212â228 (2010)." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d65126e767_5">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gechev, T. These studies have demonstrated that DT is a complex multigenic trait<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Costa, M.-C. D. et al. A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of Xerophyta viscosa. Nat. Plants 3, 17038 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d65126e774">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Gao, B. et al. De novo transcriptome characterization and gene expression profiling of the desiccation tolerant moss Bryum argenteum following rehydration. BMC Genomics 16, 416 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d65126e777">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Yobi, A. et al. Sporobolus stapfianus: Insights into desiccation tolerance in the resurrection grasses from linking transcriptomics to metabolomics. BMC Plant Biol. 2017 171 3, 1101â1108 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d65126e780">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Gechev, T. S. et al. Molecular mechanisms of desiccation tolerance in the resurrection glacial relic Haberlea rhodopensis. Cell. Mol. Life Sci. 70, 689â709 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d65126e783">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Dinakar, C. &amp; Bartels, D. Desiccation tolerance in resurrection plants: new insights from transcriptome, proteome and metabolome analysis. Front. Plant Sci. 4, 482 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d65126e786">57</a></sup>, and that there are multiple means of achieving DT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Oliver, M. J., Velten, J. &amp; Mishler, B. D. Desiccation tolerance in bryophytes: a reflection of the primitive strategy for plant survival in dehydrating habitats? Integr. Comp. Biol. 45, 788â99 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d65126e790">58</a></sup>. The genetic basis of DT, although not entirely described, may derive from regulatory differences in gene expression pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Xiao, L. et al. The resurrection genome of Boea hygrometrica: A blueprint for survival of dehydration. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 112, 5833â5837 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d65126e795">45</a></sup>, increased copy number of anahydrobiosis related genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Grene, R., Vasquez-Robinet, C. &amp; Bohnert, H. J. Molecular Biology and Physiological Genomics of Dehydration Stress. in Plant Desiccation Tolerance (eds LÃ¼ttge, U., Beck, E. &amp; Bartels, D.) 255â287 (Springer Berlin Heidelberg, 2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d65126e799">59</a></sup>, or differences in the structural organization of these genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Costa, M.-C. D. et al. A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of Xerophyta viscosa. Nat. Plants 3, 17038 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d65126e803">44</a></sup>. However, more studies are needed to resolve the specifics of DT mechanisms, and should include work on species spanning a wide phylogenetic range and degree of tolerance levels (such as the intermediate trait of dehydration tolerance (DhT also dehydration tolerant)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e807">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Oliver, M. J., Cushman, J. C. &amp; Koster, K. L. Dehydration tolerance in plants. in lant Stress Tolerance. Methods in Molecular Biology (Methods and Protocols) (ed. Sunkar, R.) 639, 3â24 (Humana Press, 2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d65126e810">60</a></sup>). <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> is DhT, which provides an important opportunity to enhance our understanding of the evolution of this intermediate trait.</p><p>Growing genomic resources for bryophytes provide novel opportunities to conduct comparative studies within these lineages, which are particularly well suited to addressing questions related to sex chromosome evolution, sex differences, and stress tolerance adaptations. Here, we aimed to characterize patterns of sequence divergence between the thalloid liverworts <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>, define genetic sex markers, and investigate the genomic basis of intraspecific variation in DhT. Our analyses indicate that the greatest sequence conservation between <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> is among chloroplast genes, likely due to the conservation of plastid function across lineages. Conversely, we show that mitochondrial genes are highly divergent, which may be related to reduced recombination of mitochondrial genomes (as observed in <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ohyama, K. Chloroplast and Mitochondrial Genomes from a Liverwort, Marchantia polymorpha âGene Organization and Molecular Evolutionâ. Biosci. Biotechnol. Biochem. 60, 16â24 (1996)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d65126e851">19</a></sup>, or variable mutation rates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Palmer, J. D. et al. Dynamic evolution of plant mitochondrial genomes: mobile genes and introns and highly variable mutation rates. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 97, 6960â6 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d65126e856">61</a></sup>. Sex-linked genes exhibit signatures of strong diversifying selection, relative to autosomal genes. Interestingly, this is driven primarily by male-specific (V) genes, which we speculate is related to strong selection on male traits to maintain species recognition. Because sperm is broadcast indiscriminately and water dispersed in <i>Marchantia</i>, pressure to maintain species recognition is expected to be particularly strong on genes related to sperm characteristics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Palumbi, S. R. All males are not created equal: fertility differences depend on gamete recognition polymorphisms in sea urchins. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 96, 12632â7 (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d65126e863">62</a></sup>. Although females could be subject similar selective pressures, our analyses indicate that selection is acting primarily on male-specific genes in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>. Putatively sex-specific sequences were identified and leveraged to develop diagnostic markers for genetic sex in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>. Regarding DhT, we detect higher copy number of dehydration associated genes in the tolerant female genotype compared to the less tolerant male genotype. Interestingly, some of these DhT genes are putatively sex-linked, offering a possible explanation for elevated DhT in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> females<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e886">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Stieha, C. R., Middleton, A. R., Stieha, J. K., Trott, S. H. &amp; Mcletchie, D. N. The dispersal process of asexual propagules and the contribution to population persistence in Marchantia (Marchantiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 101, 348â356 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65126e889">27</a></sup>. The remaining DhT genes appear to be located on autosomes and are expressed at similar levels in both sexes, which may contribute to the changing patterns of DhT that have been observed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Marks, R. A., Pike, B. &amp; Nicholas Mcletchie, D. Genetic differences in water stress tolerance track environmental exposure and exhibit a fluctuating sexual dimorphism. Rev. Oecologia." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d65126e893">63</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Whole-genome sequencing of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>, yielded 127,147,280 male reads and 133,660,960 female reads (after quality filtering). The combined male and female k-mer distribution indicated a coverage of ~24x, but showed a large quantity of unique and low abundance k-mers, suggestive of contaminating organisms (see Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S1</a>). In our efforts to characterize the source of these low abundance k-mers, we detected a diverse community of microbes, consistent with recent descriptions of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> microbial associations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Marks, R. A., Smith, J. J., Cronk, Q. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Variation in the bacteriome of the tropical liverwort, Marchantia inflexa, between the sexes and across habitats. Symbiosis 1â9, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1007/s13199-017-0522-3&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d65126e925">29</a></sup>. After removal of putative microbial sequences, we assembled the remaining sequence reads to generate the draft assembly M_inflexa_v1.1. The resulting scaffolds were assigned to super-scaffolds by alignment with the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> reference genome, allowing us to coalesce the assembly into 300 super-scaffolds. In total 7,747â<i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> scaffolds covering a total length of 81,634,927âbp were successfully mapped to the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> genome. Unmapped <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> scaffolds were appended to the supper-scaffold assembly. The resulting assembly consists of 41,556 scaffolds, covering a total of 208,839,958âbp, with and N50 of 11,144âbp and a longest scaffold length of 2,829,880âbp. This Whole Genome Shotgun project (M_inflexa_v1.1)Â has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession QLSQ00000000. The version described in this paper is version QLSQ01000000.</p><p>Assessment of assembly completeness (performed with BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d65126e957">64</a></sup>) indicated that 54.4% (783) of the 1,440 presumptively universal single-copy orthologs from the plant set of OrthoDB v9 were present in the <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> genome assembly. Another 3.5% (51) orthologs were present, but fragmented. In comparison, a parallel assessment of the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> v3.1 assembly, found that 60.2% (867) of these same genes were complete, and 2.9% (42) were fragmented in <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>. Both of these estimates are rather low, suggesting that there may be inherent limitations associated with BUSCO as has been observed for other deeply diverged lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Smith, J. J. et al. The sea lamprey germline genome provides insights into programmed genome rearrangement and vertebrate evolution. Nat. Genet. 50, 270â277 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d65126e980">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Hara, Y. et al. Optimizing and benchmarking de novo transcriptome sequencing: from library preparation to assembly evaluation. BMC Genomics 16, 977 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d65126e983">66</a></sup>. Still, we find these assessments to be informative in a comparative context within <i>Marchantia</i>.</p><p>Assembly of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> plastids generated nearly complete mitochondrial and chloroplast sequences (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Fig1">1</a>). The mitochondria of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> is 190,056âbp and the chloroplast is 122,620âbp. The complete mitochondrial and chloroplast sequences are available at FigShare (<a href="https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.6639209.v1">https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.6639209.v1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="333" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Assembled mitochondrial and chloroplast sequences of <i>Marchantia inflexa</i>. The inner circle depicts nucleotide content (G/C in dark gray and A/T in light gray). Inverted repeats (IRA and IRB) along with large (LSC) and small (SSC) single copy regions are indicated on the chloroplast inner circle. The outer circle shows annotated genes that are color coded by function. Genes located on the outside of the circle are transcribed in a clockwise direction, and those on the inside of the circle are transcribed counterclockwise.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Annotation</h3><p>Gene annotation of the <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> draft genome utilized <i>de novo</i> gene finding in combination with the lift-over of all <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> annotations for orthologous genes. Lift-over annotations from <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> to <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> resulted in the annotation of 10,005 orthologous proteins within the <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> assembly. <i>De novo</i> gene finding efforts identified 13,546 predicted proteins, 9,194 of which had identifiable orthologs across <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>patens</i>, <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>, and refseq. After removal of all redundant annotations in the <i>de novo</i> and lift-over annotations the combined set of annotations consists of 11,687 predicted proteins. Not surprisingly, the highest homology was observed between <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> with substantially less homology between <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>patens</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>, reflecting the estimated divergence times among these species (divergence time between <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> is 68â126 MYA; for <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>P</i>. <i>patens</i> it is 425â557 MYA; and for <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> it is 481â584 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Suleski, M. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree: A Resource for Timelines, Timetrees, and Divergence Times. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1812â1819 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1175">21</a></sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Sequence similarity between <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>
                        </h3><p>To investigate genome evolution within <i>Marchantia</i> we measured sequence divergence between <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>. Initially, we compared nucleotide differentiation among coding sequences (CDS), introns, and intergenic regions to estimate general patterns of divergence between lineages (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Fig2">2</a>). Comparison of orthologous CDS, introns, and intergenic sequences, revealed that (not surprisingly) intergenic sequences were the least conserved (64.5%âÂ±â0.009%), introns were intermediate (81.8%âÂ±â0.008%), and CDS were the most conserved (82.4%âÂ±â0.001%) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Fig2">2</a>). There was a significant effect of sequence type on %ID (F<sub>2,40000</sub>â=â39756, pâ&lt;â0.0001). Patterns of sequence divergence between <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> fit general expectations that CDS should exhibit higher sequence similarity compared to introns and intergenic sequences. That being said, we observed surprisingly high sequence conservation among some introns, which we speculate is related to the relatively short length of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> introns, in which functional elements (such as splice sites) may be preferentially retained.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="662" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The similarity between <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> coding sequences (CDS), introns, and intergenic sequences is shown as proportion base pairs (bp) at a % identity (%ID) ranging from 50â100%. CDS have higher overall similarity compared to introns and intergenic sequences. There was a significant effect of sequence type on %ID. Total bp of each sequence type is: CDSâ=â629,624âbp; intronsâ=â1,087âbp; and intergenicâ=â21, 983, 100âbp.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In order to assess variation in substitution rates across coding sequences, we computed the ratio of non-synonymous to synonymous mutations (dN/dS) for all orthologous CDS of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>. The resulting dN/dS values were log transformed to improve normality for statistical testing. Initially, we tested for evidence of contrasting selective pressures among autosomal, sex-linked, and organellar genes. Notably, sex linked genes and autosomes are present at 1âN, whereas copy number of the chloroplast and mitochondria is variable. We computed mean dN/dS and standard error for autosomal genes (0.24âÂ±â0.01 (nâ=â4,900)), for sex-linked genes (0.48âÂ±â0.13 (nâ=â53)), and for organellar genes (0.14âÂ±â0.03 (nâ=â116)). We detected significant differences among groups (F<sub>2,4862</sub>â=â18.54, pâ&lt;â0.001). Targeted contrasts revealed significant differences among sex-linked and autosomal genes (t<sub>1</sub>â=ââ2.38, pâ=â0.018) and among organellar and autosomal genes (t<sub>1</sub>â=â5.58, pâ=â2.6<i>e</i><sup>â8</sup>). Subsequently, we tested for differences among more specific gene types; subdividing sex-linked genes into male-specific, female-specific, and male and female alleles of genes with both U and V copies. Organellar genes were subdivided into mitochondrial and chloroplast genes. Mean dN/dS of male-specific genes was 0.63âÂ±â0.23 (nâ=â23), of female-specific was 0.20âÂ±â0.09 (nâ=â7), of male alleles was 0.24âÂ±â0.12 (nâ=â11), and of female alleles was 0.56âÂ±â0.34 (nâ=â12). Mean dN/dS of chloroplast genes was 0.03âÂ±â0.01 (nâ=â74) and of mitochondria was 0.34âÂ±â0.06 (nâ=â42). There was an overall effect of gene type on dN/dS (F<sub>2,4862</sub>â=â20.10, pâ&lt;â0.001). Targeted contrasts revealed significant differences between autosomal genes and male-specific genes (t<sub>1</sub>â=ââ2.88, pâ=â0.004), chloroplast genes (t<sub>1</sub>â=â10.39, pâ=â5<i>e</i><sup>â25</sup>), and mitochondrial genes (t<sub>1</sub>â=ââ4.40, pâ=â1.1<i>e</i><sup>â5</sup>) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Fig3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="545" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Boxplot of dN/dS values of orthologous coding sequences in <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>. There were significant differences among gene types in mean dN/dS. Secondary comparisons reveal significant differences in mean dN/dS among male-specific and autosomal genes, chloroplast and autosomal genes, and mitochondrial and autosomal genes. Statistical tests were performed on log transformed data to satisfy the assumptions of normality, but untransformed numbers are presented here. All dN/dS values &gt;â1 exhibit the signature of diversifying selection.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>These analyses reveal several genes and pathways that may be under diversifying selection (dN/dSâ&gt;â1) in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>. Sex-linked genes with dN/dSâ&gt;â1 included the female allele of CCR4-NOT transcription related complex protein (Mapoly0018s0021.1), the male bHLH-MYC2 transcription factor in the involved the jasmonate signaling pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Monte, I. et al. Ligand-receptor co-evolution shaped the jasmonate pathway in land plants. Nat. Chem. Biol. 14, 480â488 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1359">67</a></sup> (MapolyY_B0018.1), a male-specific phosphatidylinositol-4,5-bisphosphate 3-kinase (MapolyY_A0049.1), two male-specific genes of unknown function (MapolyY_B0032.1 and MapolyY_B0003.1). No chloroplast genes in our analyses had dN/dSâ&gt;â1, but three mitochondrial open reading frames (orf 84, orf 69, rpl 10) had dN/dSâ&gt;â1. Of the 243 autosomal genes with dN/dSâ&gt;â1, 51 had identifiable homologs in the Uniprot database. GO analyses of these genes revealed that many were associated with the cellular components <i>intracellular</i>, <i>cytoplasm</i>, and <i>membrane</i>, the molecular function <i>catalytic activity</i> (followed closely by <i>hydrolase activity</i> and <i>transferase activity)</i>, and the biological processes of <i>metabolic process</i> and <i>cellular process</i>. A complete list of genes with dN/dSâ&gt;â1 and associated protein names can be found as Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Sex marker identification</h3><p>We identified 4,468 regions (covering 2,234,000âbp) in the <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> genome assembly with substantial differences in copy number among genetic lines through coverage analysis with DifCover (<a href="https://github.com/timnat/DifCover">https://github.com/timnat/DifCover</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Smith, J. J. et al. The sea lamprey germline genome provides insights into programmed genome rearrangement and vertebrate evolution. Nat. Genet. 50, 270â277 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1413">65</a></sup> (see Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S2</a>). Of these, 89 were found on scaffolds also containing a predicted protein, 31 of which could be assigned to an identifiable homolog across <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>patens</i>, <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>, and refseq databases. From this set, we identified five putatively male- and three female-specific sequences that were also orthologous to sequences on the U and V chromosomes in <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>. These candidate sex markers were analyzed by PCR in nine males and nine females to verify their fidelity, leading to the validation of one positive marker for each sex (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Fig4">4</a>). Other candidate sex markers exhibited non-specific amplification and were therefore discarded. Plants used for validation were originally collected from five distinct populations, suggesting that the markers are robust to genotypic variation. Primer sequences of the validated male and female sex markers are listed in TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Tab1">1</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="348" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Electrophoresis pattern of sex markers and positive control (actin) amplified in six male and six female plants. Images shown here are sections cropped from two separate gels (see Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S5</a> for full length gel images). Male and female markers are presumptive U- and V-linked sequences, respectively. Plants were originally collected from five natural populations spanning a range of environmental conditions. In total, sex markers were validated in nine male and nine female plants, but only six individuals of each sex are shown here.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Primer sequences for validated male and female genetic sex markers for <i>Marchantia inflexa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Dehydration tolerance</h3><p>To address specific hypotheses on DhT we probed <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> annotated proteins for orthologs to a list of 195 DT genes (compiled from publicly available mRNA sequences of genes expressed under water stress in model DT plants). See Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S2</a> for the accession numbers, species, and studies from which genes were compiled. Of this set of DT genes, 112 had identifiable homologs in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and 141 had identifiable homologs in <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>. Our analyses of dN/dS captured 38 of these DT orthologs, one of which (a putative aldehyde dehydrogenase (Mapoly0030s0099.1)) had a dN/dS valueâ&gt;â1. The function of diversification in this gene is unclear, given the lack of evidence for any difference in DhT between these two <i>Marchantia</i> species.</p><p>Prior studies showed that the male and female <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> genotypes used for genome assembly have reproducible differences in DhT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1607">26</a></sup>. Consequently, we aimed to identify DT genes with substantial coverage differences among these two genotypes, presuming that they may impact relative differences in DhT. Of the 112 DT genes detected in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>, most had standardized coverage ratios of ~1. However, six genes had considerably higher coverage (log<sub>2</sub> fold changeâ&gt;â|4|) in the highly tolerant female and one had higher coverage in the less tolerant male (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Tab2">2</a>). Specific genes with higher coverage in the tolerant female genotype include a calcium dependent protein kinase (CDPK), glucose related protein 94 (GRP94), the aldehyde dehydrogenase (ALDH) (also identified in dN/dS analyses above), heat shock proteins 70 and 101 (HSP101, HSP70), and superoxide dismutase (SOD). The sole DT gene with higher coverage in the less tolerant male genotype is a heat shock factor 1 (HSF 1). Of the DT genes with coverage difference among genotypes, one (CDPK) was assigned to the putative U chromosome, and one (HSF 1) was assigned to the putative V chromosome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 DhT proteins with coverage differences among the sexes in <i>Marchantia inflexa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To verify that candidate DT associated genes were expressed during dehydration and to test for sex-specific patterns of expression, we conducted qPCR validation of seven candidate dehydration associated genes. We quantified expression for each gene in three males and three females under both hydrated and dehydrated conditions by qPCR. These analyses revealed that all candidate genes were expressed in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> under both hydrated and dehydrated conditions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Fig5">5</a>). Analysis of changes in relative expression of DhT associated genes during dehydration identified an overall increase in expression during dehydration (F<sub>1,1</sub>â=â5.70, pâ=â0.019), differences among genes (F<sub>6,6</sub>â=â9.03, pâ&lt;â0.001) and a significant interaction between gene and hydration state (F<sub>6,6</sub>â=â2.41, pâ=â0.0351). The interaction effect was driven primarily by an increase in HSP70 expression during dehydration (other genes did not show significant changes in expression during dehydration). Interestingly, the two candidate genes that were putatively sex-linked (female-specific CDPK and male-specific HSF 1) exhibited sex-specific expression, but autosomal candidate DhT genes (GRP94, ALDH, HSP101, HSP70, and SOD) were expressed at similar levels in males and females suggesting copies are present in both sexes. These autosomal genes were present at different copy number in the male and female used for genome assembly, but in the larger panel assayed by qPCR this did not translate to differences in relative expression among the sexes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-45039-9/MediaObjects/41598_2019_45039_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="540" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Relative expression of candidate dehydration associated genes in three male and three female plants under hydrated and dehydrated conditions. Gene expression estimates were standardized using an internal control gene (actin). The expression of each candidate gene relative to actin was computed to estimate relative abundance. Absence of a bar (as seen for HSF 1 and CDPK) indicates that there was no amplification of that gene in the corresponding sample. Error bars are standard error of the mean.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Our assembly of the <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> genome represents a new resource for comparative studies among land plants. We capitalized on the recently published genome of related liverwort <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304.e15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1828">2</a></sup> to improve scaffolding of our assembly, estimate divergence rates among specific sequence types, and to identify sex-linked sequences that were leveraged to generate male and female genetic sex markers for <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>.
