Our analyses identify several genes on the autosomes, organelles, and sex chromosomes that show strong signatures of recent diversifying selection in <i>Marchantia</i>. Additionally, we identified multiple genes possibly underlying an observed genotype difference in DhT in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>, which point towards a complex mechanism of heightened DhT. We detected differences in copy number of DhT genes across multiple loci, two of which were putatively sex-linked. Evidence of sex-linked genes underlying differences in DhT is intriguing, as prior studies indicate complex patterns of sexual dimorphism in DhT in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1854">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Marks, R. A., Pike, B. &amp; Nicholas Mcletchie, D. Genetic differences in water stress tolerance track environmental exposure and exhibit a fluctuating sexual dimorphism. Rev. Oecologia." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1857">63</a></sup>.</p><p>Analyses of dN/dS ratios for genes on autosomes, sex chromosomes, and organelles in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> showed evidence of increased diversification of sex-linked genes relative to autosomal genes, and conservation of organellar genes (particularly the chloroplast) relative to autosomes. UV sex determination systems are expected to differ from diploid dominant (XY and ZW) sex determination systems in multiple ways, due primarily to haploid selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bachtrog, D. et al. Are all sex chromosomes created equal? Trends Genet. 27, 350â357 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1876">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Bull, J. J. Sex Chromosomes in Haploid Dioecy: A Unique Contrast to Mullerâs Theory for Diploid Dioecy. Am. Nat. 112, 245â250 (1978)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1879">37</a></sup>. However, both empirical and theoretical studies on sex chromosome evolution in haploid dominant systems are limited. Some UV sex chromosomes exhibit suppressed recombination similar to XY and ZW sex chromosomes, which can lead to degeneration of UV chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Immler, S. &amp; Otto, S. P. The evolution of sex chromosomes in organisms with separate haploid sexes. Evolution (N. Y). 69, 694â708 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1884">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Bull, J. J. Sex Chromosomes in Haploid Dioecy: A Unique Contrast to Mullerâs Theory for Diploid Dioecy. Am. Nat. 112, 245â250 (1978)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1887">37</a></sup>. However, because UV chromosomes are fully exposed to selection during the majority of the organismâs life cycle, positive adaptations may sweep through populations more rapidly than in XY or and ZW systems. Our analyses indicate that rapid diversification of sex-linked genes is occurring in <i>Marchantia</i>, suggesting that exposure to haploid selection can be a diversifying force on sex chromosomes.</p><p>Interestingly, the finding that <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> sex-linked genes are more divergent than autosomal genes is driven primarily by diversification of male-specific (V) genes. Diversification of female-specific (U) genes, and genes with alleles on both the U and V chromosomes is similar to diversification of autosomal genes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#Fig3">3</a>). It has been suggested that UV chromosomes can become highly differentiated between the sexes due to sex-specific selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Immler, S. &amp; Otto, S. P. The evolution of sex chromosomes in organisms with separate haploid sexes. Evolution (N. Y). 69, 694â708 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1913">36</a></sup>. Males are generally thought to be under stronger sexual selection than females, and variability in male reproductive success may facilitate rapid adaptation of V-linked genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bachtrog, D. et al. Are all sex chromosomes created equal? Trends Genet. 27, 350â357 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1917">35</a></sup>. Further because male gametes are broadcast indiscriminately, we expect strong selection on sperm characteristics to maintain species recognition in this system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Palumbi, S. R. All males are not created equal: fertility differences depend on gamete recognition polymorphisms in sea urchins. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 96, 12632â7 (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1921">62</a></sup>. Evidence for diversification of multiple male-specific genes provides a possible mechanism for maintaining species boundaries.</p><p>Sex-linked genes with high dN/dS appear to be involved in multiple cellular processes, some of which contribute to differences in sex function in other systems. Notably, the female-allele of CCR4-NOT transcription related complex (dN/dSâ=â4.27) is homologous to the <i>rcd1</i><sup>+</sup> gene of <i>Schizosaccharomyces pombe</i>, which is critical for nitrogen starvation induced sexual reproduction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Okazaki, N. et al. Novel factor highly conserved among eukaryotes controls sexual development in fission yeast. Mol. Cell. Biol. 18, 887â95 (1998)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1935">68</a></sup>. Divergence of these genes within <i>Marchantia</i> points towards specific reproductive differences among these species, which may be related to selection for species-specific recognition, habitat-specific optimization, or the evolutionary dynamics of haploid selection on sex chromosomes.</p><p>Our analyses define male and female-specific genetic sex markers, which will be of great utility in future studies of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> populations where plants are latent to express sex. The ability to sex plants via a simple PCR assay will expedite general efforts to characterize individuals and populations of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Marks, R. A., Pike, B. &amp; Nicholas Mcletchie, D. Genetic differences in water stress tolerance track environmental exposure and exhibit a fluctuating sexual dimorphism. Rev. Oecologia." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1957">63</a></sup>, and will aid in efforts to develop <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> as a model system to investigate population dynamics and ecological genetics.</p><p>In our efforts to identify genes underlying DhT we capitalized on a previously identified genotype difference in DhT in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1976">26</a></sup>, targeting genes that exhibited substantial coverage differences among genotypes. We identified several such genes, which we speculated contribute to differences in DhT among genotypes. Interestingly two of these genes were putatively sex-linked, suggesting that there may be sex-specific components to DhT in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>. Importantly, qPCR validation of seven candidate dehydration associated genes in additional male and female plants revealed persistent expression of these genes under hydrated and dehydrated conditions, supporting a possible role for these genes in DhT. The unique biology of bryophytes makes them prone to rapid drying and thus, many genes involved in DhT are expressed prior to dehydration in bryophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Proctor, M. C. F. et al. Desiccation-tolerance in bryophytes: a review Desiccation-tolerance in bryophytes: a review. Bryologist 110, 595â621 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1986">43</a></sup>. Various patterns of expression have been observed in DhT and DT bryophytes, including constitutive expression, expression induced during drying, and the sequestration of mRNAs in ribonucleoprotein particles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Wood, A. J. &amp; Oliver, M. J. Translational control in plant stress: the formation of messenger ribonucleoprotein particles (mRNPs) in response to desiccation of Tortula ruralis gametophytes. Plant J. 18, 359â370 (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1991">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Scott, H. B. &amp; Oliver, M. J. Accumulation and polysomal recruitment of transcripts in response to desiccation and rehydration of the moss Tortula ruralis. J. Exp. Bot. 45, 577â583 (1994)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d65126e1994">70</a></sup>. Thus, there is no absolute predication for how a given gene will be expressed during dehydration. Our qPCR analyses suggest that even in this small set of candidate DhT genes, multiple patterns of expression are evident in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>. The majority of tested genes were expressed constitutively, but one (HSP70) showed a significant increase in expression during dehydration. Importantly, the two genes that were putatively sex-linked (female-specific CDPK and male-specific HSF1) were expressed in a strictly sex-specific pattern. Interestingly CDPKs have been recognized as important hubs in plant stress signaling pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Schulz, P., Herde, M. &amp; Romeis, T. Calcium-Dependent Protein Kinases: Hubs in Plant Stress Signaling and Development. Plant Physiol. 163 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2004">71</a></sup> with highly conserved structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Cheng, S.-H., Willmann, M. R., Chen, H.-C. &amp; Sheen, J. Calcium Signaling through Protein Kinases. The Arabidopsis Calcium-Dependent Protein Kinase Gene Family. Plant Physiol. 129 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2008">72</a></sup>, which provides a possible explanation for elevated DhT in females. Other candidate DhT genes were expressed at similar levels across the sexes, suggesting that despite possible involvement in DhT these genes are unlikely to drive sex-related differences in DhT within <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>. Quantification of candidate DhT gene expression by qPCR further reinforces gene annotations in demonstrating that predicted <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> genes are expressed in the expected tissues and individuals.</p><p>In summary, the draft genome for <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> adds to a growing body of genomic resources for land plants, which will enable investigation of early plant evolution and physiology. We leveraged this assembly to identify genes under diversifying selection in <i>Marchantia</i>, to develop genetic sex markers, and to target genes contributing to DhT. Our analyses comprise one of the few empirical studies on haploid sex chromosome evolution and suggest that several sex-linked genes (particularly male-specific (V) genes) have undergone rapid diversification in <i>Marchantia</i>. We identified multiple sex-specific sequences, which were used to develop genetic sex markers and identify genes underlying differences in DhT of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>. We find evidence that DhT in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> is likely impacted by the constitutive expression of select DhT genes, and that sex differences in DhT may be impacted by sex-linked DhT genes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant growth, DNA extraction, and sequencing</h3><p>Plant specimens for genome sequencing were collected from East Turure stream (10Â°41â²04â³N 61Â°09â²39â³W) on the island of Trinidad, Republic of Trinidad and Tobago in 2009. Voucher specimens are deposited at the Missouri Botanical Garden (St. Louis, MO, USA, specimen numbers M092113 and M092115) and at the National Herbarium of the Republic of Trinidad and Tobago (St. Augustine, Trinidad, specimen number TRIN34616, D. N. McLetchie, collector). Vegetative tissue was transported to Lexington, Kentucky, USA and thirty-six clones (generated though vegetative propagation) of one male and one female genotype were planted on steam-sterilized soil and maintained in a randomized layout in a climate-controlled greenhouse. Plants were watered daily with distilled water and kept under shade cloth to mimic field conditions. Vegetative tissue (growing aerially with no soil contact) was collected from male and female plants after ~5 years in greenhouse conditions. Prior to DNA extraction, thalli were washed in distilled water three times to remove surface contamination. DNA was extracted following a CTAB extraction protocol modified from Doyle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Doyle, J. L. A rapid DNA isolation procedure for small quantities of fresh leaf tissue. Phytochem. Bull. 19, 11â15 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2065">73</a></sup>. Sequencing libraries were constructed with 300 base pair (bp) inserts and whole genome sequencing was conducted on an Illumina HiSeq2000 for 100âbp paired end (PE) reads at the Beaty Biodiversity Research Centre, University of British Columbia.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Sequence read quality was assessed with fastQC version 3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Andrews, S. FastQC A Quality Control tool for High Throughput Sequence Data. (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2077">74</a></sup>, and filtered with Trimmomatic version 0.33<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â20 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2081">75</a></sup>. Male and female reads were combined to increase coverage and k-mer plots were generated with DSK version 1.1 (see Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Rizk, G., Lavenier, D. &amp; Chikhi, R. DSK: k-mer counting with very low memory usage. Bioinformatics 29, 652â3 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2088">76</a></sup>. Assembly was carried out using SOAP <i>de novo</i> version 2.04-r240<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2096">77</a></sup> with a k-mer length of 31. Reads shorter than 100âbp were not included, alignments of less than 32âbp were not considered reliable, and k-mers observed nine or fewer times were excluded from the assembly.</p><p>Following initial assembly, we plotted the length and GC content of each scaffold in JMPÂ®, Version 12 (SAS Institute Inc.). The plot revealed two distinct clusters of well-assembled (long) scaffolds: one with a mean GC content of ~65% and one with a mean GC content of ~45% (see Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S3</a>). Consequently, we probed each distinct GC cluster to identify the taxonomic source of the contributing sequence reads by aligning the 100 longest scaffolds of each GC cluster to NCBIâs refseq database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Pruitt, K. D., Tatusova, T. &amp; Maglott, D. R. NCBI Reference Sequence (RefSeq): a curated non-redundant sequence database of genomes, transcripts and proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D501â4 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2106">78</a></sup> using TBLASTX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â10 (1990)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2110">79</a></sup>. Taxonomic classification of the resulting alignments with Megan version 4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Huson, D. H., Auch, A. F., Qi, J. &amp; Schuster, S. C. MEGAN analysis of metagenomic data. Genome Res. 17, 377â86 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2114">80</a></sup> revealed that scaffolds with high GC content were derived from a diverse microbial community, whereas scaffolds with low GC content were derived exclusively from plant material (see Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S4</a>). Notably, other members of <i>Marchantia</i> have a GC content of ~45%<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Sharma, N., Jung, C.-H., Bhalla, P. L. &amp; Singh, M. B. RNA Sequencing Analysis of the Gametophyte Transcriptome from the Liverwort, Marchantia polymorpha. PLoS One 9, e97497 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2125">81</a></sup>, providing additional support for the assumption that low GC content reads were derived from <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>. Consequently, we filtered the raw sequence data to remove all reads with a GC content &gt;55%. The remaining reads (although likely not entirely contamination free) represent a data set enriched for <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> genomic information. Using only reads with a GC content &lt;55%, we reassembled the sequence data with the same parameters as above.</p><p>The resulting scaffolds were aligned to the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304.e15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2150">2</a></sup> with BLASTN, allowing us to organize <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> scaffolds with Chromosomer version 1.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Tamazian, G. et al. Chromosomer: a reference-based genome arrangement tool for producing draft chromosome sequences. Gigascience 5, 38 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2161">82</a></sup>, which leverages pairwise sequence alignments and local synteny to assign orthologous regions to super-scaffolds. All <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> scaffolds not mapped to <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> were appended to the super-scaffold assembly, and all contigs under 1000âbp were removed. Assembly statistics were computed using assemblathon_stats_2.pl script (<a href="https://github.com/lexnederbragt/denovo-assembly-tutorial/blob/master/scripts/assemblathon_stats_2.pl">https://github.com/lexnederbragt/denovo-assembly-tutorial/blob/master/scripts/assemblathon_stats_2.pl</a>). To estimate assembly completeness, we quantified the percentage of Universal Single-copy Orthologs from the plant set of OrthoDB v9 of BUSCO v3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2185">64</a></sup> present in the <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> genome assembly. We conducted a parallel assessment of the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> genome assembly.</p><p>To assemble the mitochondrial and chloroplast genomes of <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> raw reads were trimmed with Trimmomatic version 0.33<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â20 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2210">75</a></sup> and error corrected using the ErrorCorrectReads module of Allpaths-LG version 50156<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 108, 1513â1518 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2214">83</a></sup>. These were aligned to the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> reference plastid and mitochondrial sequences (GI 11466673<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Ohyama, K. et al. Structure and organization of Marchantia polymorpha chloroplast genome. I. Cloning and gene identification. J. Mol. Biol. 203, 281â98 (1988)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2225">84</a></sup> and GI 11467097<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Oda, K. et al. Gene organization deduced from the complete sequence of liverwort Marchantia polymorpha mitochondrial DNA. A primitive form of plant mitochondrial genome. J. Mol. Biol. 223, 1â7 (1992)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2229">11</a></sup>, respectively) with BWA mem version 0.7.12-r1039<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. https://doi.org/arXiv:1303.3997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2233">85</a></sup>. Reads with alignments, plus their mates, were extracted and partitioned. Each partition was assembled separately with Ray de novo version 2.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Boisvert, S., Laviolette, F. &amp; Corbeil, J. Ray: Simultaneous Assembly of Reads from a Mix of High-Throughput Sequencing Technologies. J. Comput. Biol. 17, 1519â1533 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2237">86</a></sup> at kâ=â31. Ray contigs were scaffolded against their homologous <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> reference sequences using Abacas version 1.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Assefa, S., Keane, T. M., Otto, T. D., Newbold, C. &amp; Berriman, M. ABACAS: algorithm-based automatic contiguation of assembled sequences. Bioinformatics 25, 1968â1969 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2248">87</a></sup>. Adjacent contig overlaps were identified using BLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389â402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2252">88</a></sup> and merged at the shared substring. Pilon version 1.13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: An Integrated Tool for Comprehensive Microbial Variant Detection and Genome Assembly Improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2256">89</a></sup> was then run for thirty iterations. Assemblies were annotated with Daisie Huangâs script PLANN (<a href="https://github.com/daisieh/plann">https://github.com/daisieh/plann</a>) and visualized in OrganellarGenomeDRAW (<a href="http://ogdraw.mpimp-golm.mpg.de/cgi-bin/ogdraw.pl">http://ogdraw.mpimp-golm.mpg.de/cgi-bin/ogdraw.pl</a>).</p><p>We used Crossmap version 2.7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Zhao, H. et al. CrossMap: a versatile tool for coordinate conversion between genome assemblies. Bioinformatics 30, 1006â1007 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2278">90</a></sup> to transfer all <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> gene annotations to orthologous <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> sequences. In addition to these lift-over annotations, <i>de novo</i> gene prediction was carried out via Maker version 2.31.8<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188â96 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2298">91</a></sup> for four iterations of gene finding. The resulting predicted proteins were aligned to the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304.e15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2308">2</a></sup>, <i>Physcometrella patens</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rensing, S. A. et al. The Physcomitrella genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science (80-.). 319, 64â9 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2316">7</a></sup>, the <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Berardini, T. Z. et al. The Arabidopsis information resource: Making and mining the âgold standardâ annotated reference plant genome. Genesis 53, 474â85 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2323">92</a></sup>, and NCBIâs refseq database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Pruitt, K. D., Tatusova, T. &amp; Maglott, D. R. NCBI Reference Sequence (RefSeq): a curated non-redundant sequence database of genomes, transcripts and proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D501â4 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2327">78</a></sup> using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â10 (1990)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2331">79</a></sup>. All alignments were merged and the single best hit for each <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> predicted protein was selected based on bitscore. The combined set of <i>de novo</i> and lift-over annotations was used for downstream analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Sequence similarity between <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>
                        </h3><p>To enable comparison of orthologous sequences, we aligned the entire <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> assembly to the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> assembly v3.1 with LASTZ version 1.04<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Harris, R. S. Improved pairwise alignment of genomic DNA. (Pennsylvania State University, 2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2378">93</a></sup>, and extracted orthologous CDS, introns, and intergenic sequences from both assemblies using a combination of BEDtools version 2.19.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841â842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2383">94</a></sup> and BEDOPS version 2.4.35<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Neph, S. et al. BEDOPS: high-performance genomic feature operations. Bioinformatics 28, 1919â1920 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2387">95</a></sup>. To explicitly test for differences in nucleotide differentiation among these sequence types, CDS, introns, and intergenic sequences were realigned to one another using LASTZ version 1.04. The resulting mean % identity (%ID) for each sequence type was computed and differences among sequence types were tested for significance with a mixed effects linear model in JMPÂ®, Version 12 (SAS Institute Inc.). The fixed effect of sequence type on %ID was tested (sequence length was included in the model as a random effect).</p><p>To investigate patterns of gene divergence we computed dN/dS for all orthologous CDS in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i>. Initially, we extracted the complete CDS and translated amino acid sequence for all orthologous genes using gffread (<a href="https://github.com/gpertea/gffread">https://github.com/gpertea/gffread</a>). Orthologous translated CDS were aligned with Clustal Omega version 1.2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Sievers, F. et al. Fast, scalable generation of high-quality protein multiple sequence alignments using Clustal Omega. Mol. Syst. Biol. 7, 539 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2414">96</a></sup>, and codon aware DNA alignments were defined using PAL2NAL version 14<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, 609â12 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2418">97</a></sup>, during which all gaps and internal stop codons were removed. Next, dN/dS ratios for each ortholog were calculated with the yn00 function of PAML version 4.9<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: Phylogenetic Analysis by Maximum Likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2422">98</a></sup>, which computes dN/dS using pairwise comparisons accounting for both transition/transversion bias and base/codon frequency bias<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Yang, Z. &amp; Nielsen, R. Estimating Synonymous and Nonsynonymous Substitution Rates Under Realistic Evolutionary Models. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 32â43 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2426">99</a></sup>. Following filtering conventions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Villanueva-CaÃ±as, J. L., Laurie, S. &amp; AlbÃ , M. M. Improving genome-wide scans of positive selection by using protein isoforms of similar length. Genome Biol. Evol. 5, 457â67 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2430">100</a></sup>, cases in which dSâ=â0, dN &gt;2, and dN/dS &gt;10 were removed from the output, and dN/dS values were log transformed to satisfy assumptions of normality for statistical testing. Differences among groups in mean dN/dS were tested for significance using a mixed effects linear model in JMPÂ®, Version 12 (SAS Institute Inc.). Initially, the fixed effect of gene type (autosomal, sex-linked, or organellar) on dN/dS was tested (scaffold ID was included in the model as a random effect). Post hoc comparisions among gene types were made using orthogonal contrasts to explicitly compare autosomal genes to sex and organallar genes. Subesquntly, we made more detailed comparisions among specific gene types with a mixed effects linear model testing the fixed effect of specific gene type (autosomal, male-specific, female-specific, male-allele, female-allele, mitochondria, and chloroplast) on dN/dS (scaffold ID was included in the model as a random effect). Again, post hoc comparisons were made using orthogonal contrasts to specifically compare each gene type to the autosomal genes. Finally, all individual genes with dN/dS valuesâ&gt;1 were identified, and gene ontology (GO) terms were defined with the GORetreiver tool and summarized with the GOSlimViewer tool available at AgBase (<a href="http://agbase.msstate.edu/cgi-bin/tools.pl">http://agbase.msstate.edu/cgi-bin/tools.pl</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="McCarthy, F. M. et al. AgBase: a functional genomics resource for agriculture. BMC Genomics 7, 229 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2442">101</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Sex marker identification</h3><p>Read coverage was computed using DifCover (<a href="https://github.com/timnat/DifCover">https://github.com/timnat/DifCover</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Smith, J. J. et al. The sea lamprey germline genome provides insights into programmed genome rearrangement and vertebrate evolution. Nat. Genet. 50, 270â277 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2461">65</a></sup> to identify regions of the genome unique to these male and female genotypes. Briefly, we determined the genotype-specific coverage by mapping male and female sequence reads back to the draft assembly with Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2465">102</a></sup>. Coverage was calculated for 500âbp windows with BEDtools version 2.19.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841â842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2469">94</a></sup>, and DifCover was used to calculate the log<sub>2</sub> ratios of male:female coverage for each 500âbp window. <i>Marchantia inflexa</i> sequences that were both homologous to the <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> sex chromosomes and showed genotype-specific coverage were flagged as potential sex markers. PCR primers were designed with primer3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Untergasser, A. et al. Primer3ânew capabilities and interfaces. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e115 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2485">103</a></sup> for five candidate male markers and three candidate female markers.</p><p>Candidate sex markers were tested for fidelity by PCR analysis using plants from five distinct populations in Trinidad in 2016 (see Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S3</a> for sample collection info). Plants were cultivated in greenhouse conditions on steam-sterilized soil, under shade cloth, and watered daily for ~one year. When plants began to produce sex organs naturally, vegetative tissue (visibly connected to a reproductive structure to ensure accurate sex identification) was collected from nine individuals of each sex. DNA was extracted from plant tissues following a modified CTAB extraction protocol (same as above), and PCR reactions were conducted with a DNA template concentration of 0.8âng/ul and combined forward and reverse primer concentration of 0.4 uM. Reaction conditions consisted of initial denaturation at 94âÂ°C for five minutes, followed by 34 cycles of 94âÂ°C for 30âseconds, 60âÂ°C for 30âseconds, 72âÂ°C for 15âseconds, and a final extension at 72âÂ°C for five minutes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Dehydration tolerance</h3><p>To address specific hypotheses on DhT we probed <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> annotated proteins for orthologs to a list of 195 DT genes (compiled from publicly available mRNA sequences of genes expressed under water stress in model DT plants (see Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S2</a>)). DT orthologs were identified using BLASTX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â10 (1990)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2520">79</a></sup>, and the single best hits for each <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>polymorpha</i> sequence were determined based on bitscore. Presuming that some DT genes would be multi-copy in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i>, we calculated the genotype-specific coverage of each DhT ortholog using DifCover<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Smith, J. J. et al. The sea lamprey germline genome provides insights into programmed genome rearrangement and vertebrate evolution. Nat. Genet. 50, 270â277 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2543">65</a></sup> with the aim of detecting genes contributing to the observed genotype difference in DhT in <i>M</i>. <i>inflexa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2552">26</a></sup>. We targeted sequences corresponding to DhT genes showing log<sub>2</sub> coverage ratiosâ&gt;â|4| as potential contributors to the observed difference in DhT. Finally, we computed dN/dS ratios for all DhT genes and identified those exhibiting signs of diversifying selection (dN/dSâ&gt;â1).</p><p>To verify that candidate dehydration associated genes were expressed during dehydration and to test for sex-specific patterns of expression, we conducted qPCR validation of seven candidate genes. To do so, we selected three male and three female plants from East Turure stream (see Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S3</a>). Plants were cultivated in a common garden for ~eight years prior to being subjected to dehydration treatment as described in<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Marks, R. A., Burton, J. F. &amp; McLetchie, D. N. Sex differences and plasticity in dehydration tolerance: insight from a tropical liverwort. Ann. Bot. 118, 347â56 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2564">26</a></sup>. Briefly, thallus tissues (~5âmmâÃâ7âmm) were harvested from greenhouse cultivated plants, fully hydrated for 24âhours, and placed into desiccation chambers with an internal relative humidity (RH) of 75%. Thallus tips were randomized in Petri dishes within in the desiccation chamber. Each desiccation chamber contained 18 thallus tips (three tips from each of the six genotypes). Air circulation was maintained by inserting a small fan in the chamber, and RH was verified with a RH sensor integrated into HOBO<b>â¢</b> humidity sensor attached to a data logger (Onset Computer Corporation, Bourne, MA, USA). The chamber was maintained at 14âÂ°C for 22âhours. Dehydration assays were conducted at designated times of day to reduce off target variation due to fluctuations in light, temperature, and circadian rhythms.</p><p>Plants were sampled at two time points during the dehydration assay: initial hydrated conditions and dehydrated conditions (22âhours). At each time point, tissues were removed from the desiccation chamber and immediately flash frozen in liquid nitrogen to prevent further transcriptional changes, RNA was extracted from samples using the Triazol<sup>Â®</sup>Reagent according to the manufacturerâs instructions, mRNA was isolated through poly(A) enrichment with the NEBNextÂ® Poly(A) mRNA Magnetic Isolation Module, and cDNA synthesis was conducted with SMARTScribeâ¢ Reverse Transcriptase using random primers. Biological replicates (genotypes) were processed independently and randomized for all downstream processing and analyses to minimize possible batch effects.</p><p>We measured gene expression for seven dehydration associated genes (GRP94, ALDH, HSP101, HSP70, SOD, female-specific CDPK, and male-specific HSF 1) by qPCR. The housekeeping gene actin was included as an internal control. Primer pairs for each gene were designed using Primer3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Untergasser, A. et al. Primer3ânew capabilities and interfaces. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e115 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d65126e2579">103</a></sup> and are listed Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S4</a>. qPCR reactions were carried out in 96 well plates on a Roche LightCycler<sup>Â®</sup> 96. The reaction mix consisted of 1ul cDNA, 0.5ul each of the forward and reverse primers (10uM), 10ul LunaÂ® Universal qPCR Master Mix, and 8ul PCR-grade H<sub>2</sub>O, for a final reaction volume of 20ul. A template free control was included for each primer pair. The amplification program consisted of a 60âsecond preincubation at 95âÂ°C, followed by 45 cycles of 95âÂ°C for 5âseconds, 60âÂ°C for 15âseconds, and 72âÂ°C for 10âseconds. Following amplification, high resolution melting analyses was conducted, via one cycle of 95âÂ°C for 60âseconds, 40âÂ°C for 60âseconds, 65âÂ°C for 1âsecond, and 97âÂ°C for 1âsecond.</p><p>Data were analyzed using the Roche LightCycler<sup>Â®</sup> 96 Software Version 1.1. Initially, melting curve analysis was conducted to confirm product quality, and any samples with abnormal profiles were removed from the analyses. Raw Cq (quantitation cycle) values were used to estimate cDNA concentration for the seven candidate dehydration associated genes and housekeeping gene actin (used as an internal control) (see Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-45039-9#MOESM1">S6</a>). Cq indicates the first cycle at which fluorescence could be detected (smaller Cq indicates a higher starting concentration of the transcript). Subsequently, we computed the abundance of each candidate gene relative to the internal control gene actin. These analyses were performed according to the instructions for relative quantification analyses in the Roche LightCycler<sup>Â®</sup> 96 manual to determine the ratio of target cDNA to actin (to account for potential differences in the starting concentration of cDNA among samples) based on raw Cq values and estimated cDNA concentrations. To test for changes in expression during dehydration and investigate sex differences in DhT gene expression, the relative ratio of target cDNA to actin (internal control gene) was analyzed in JMPÂ®, Version 12 (SAS Institute Inc.). We used a mixed effects linear model to test the fixed effects of gene, hydration state, sex, and all second and third order interaction effects on the relative ratio of target:control expression. Contrasts were used to test for changes in expression during dehydration of each individual gene.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data Availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data Availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The unprocessed sequence data associated with this study are available in GenBank (accession numbers: SRR7348360 and SRR7348361). This Whole Genome Shotgun project (M_inflexa_v1.1)Â has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession QLSQ00000000. The version described in this paper is version QLSQ01000000. The complete mitochondrial and chloroplast sequences are available at FigShare (<a href="https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.6639209.v1">https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.6639209.v1</a>).</p>
